value="1"></gd:extendedproperty><gd:extendedproperty name="commentModerationMode" value="FILTERED_POSTMOD"></gd:extendedproperty></entry><entry><id>tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-8545641.post-4309108753175363519</id><published>2015-05-12T17:00:00.000-04:00</published><updated>2015-05-12T17:00:01.714-04:00</updated><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="1969"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="1970"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Back to Basics"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Beatles"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Country Rock"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Folk Rock"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Music"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Rock and roll"></category><title type="text">Let it Be (1970) by the Beatles</title><content type="html">&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;After years of being the most pioneering studio band in the world - or one of the two - the Beatles originally decided they would go back to basics and record an album in the manner of &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Please Please&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt; &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Me&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;; that is, live in the studio. To that end they actually re-posed for its cover shot approximately six years after the original. (It eventually became the cover of one of their best-of compilations, &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;The Blue Album.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;)&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;a name='more'&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;The idea involved an accompanying film, which turned out would wreak havoc with the attempts at putting out the album. The Beatles hired then up-and-coming Glyn Johns as the engineer - he would go on to &lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: #000081; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;engineer and co-produce some major records in the '70s &lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;- but implying when he was hired that he was also the producer. They never informed Martin or Johns what their roles would be  were they coproducing with each other or what? Martin assumed he was the producer, as he always was. And Johns assumed he was the producer, because he had been led to believe that. Additionally the Beatles tried to rehearse in a film studio specifically so the film crew could work around them. That didn't work: Harrison quit at the very beginning of the sessions - for the second time in only a few months  because of the recording conditions.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;The Beatles then attempted to record in the studios they had acquired with their new label, Apple. Harrison claimed that Clapton's presence on While My Guitar Gently Weeps had made the band behave like humans to each other. So, to this end he hired Billy Preston to play keyboards for the sessions  once he had returned to the band. (This served a dual purpose since the band was without a keyboard player most of the time, if they were going to play live.) The band played an enormous amount of material - though often failed to complete the performances - during these rehearsals and recording sessions, including numerous covers - as part of the back to our roots approach. Only a little of it has surfaced officially on &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Let It Be &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;and its re-release, &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Naked&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;, and on &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Anthology&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;, although there are apparently numerous bootlegs of the sessions.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;The seriousness of the back-to-basics approach can be seen in the &lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;infamous rooftop concert shut down by police, that instantly became iconic and has inspired numerous surprise performances by major acts throughout the ensuing years. That a band who had been screamed out of performing live a few years earlier would embrace performing live again, if only to try to save something as it was falling apart, shows either how committed they were  at the time  to the idea of a complete rejection of their pioneering studio trickery, or how desperate they were to capture the good old days. It was probably a mixture of both.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;There was no such thing as a warts-and-all / back-to-basics album before the attempt at &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;. Until the invention of multi-tracking in the 1950s, it was a technical impossibility, let alone something that anyone would ever think of. And until psychedelia emphasized the excesses of multi-tracking in 1966 and '67, why would anyone want to forsake new technology that made you sound better? The concept was still novel in 1969. Even though the Byrds went back to basics by making country music in mid 1968, they did not give up studio technology, going so far as to overdub Gram Parsons out of the band after he quit. When the Rolling Stones abandoned psychedelia almost as quickly as they took it up, to re-embrace their first loves in late 1968 - and the Indian instruments on a song don't indicate a full abandonment of psychedelia - they did not forsake multi-tracking. Even CCR, the inventors of roots rock, overdubbed keyboards and extra guitar parts regularly. And if the Flamin Groovies recorded live in the studio, nobody even knew they existed so who cares what they did. &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;The album, as Ive outlined above, was ready to go; everything had been recorded. More than enough had been recorded. The problem was that the Beatles disagreed on how it should be mixed, or whether it was even releasable. McCartney wanted to release it whereas the others seemed somewhat embarrassed. They would continue to fight over it for over a year; with various versions of the album being prepared to the point where copies were almost pressed a few times. Incidentally, McCartney's interest in releasing the warts-and-all, original version of &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;should dispel any notions of he was always about glossy, easy pop.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;So the Beatles  even though they failed in their attempt  insinuated the idea of a return to the roots as a remedy for studio excesses into the rock and roll consciousness. Zeppelin released the nearly overdub-free &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Presence &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;halfway through their career. The Clash tried to do it with &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Cut the Crap&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;. There have been numerous other attempts. It is now a critics' clich; it has basically become part of the lexicon, just like any band that is really ambitious has to attempt a &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;White Album &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;style kitchen sink thing - on one CD or two. If a band gets totally out of control in the studio, the reaction of everyone is that they should go back to basics - even if the result rarely is overdub free. And this comes from the Beatles attempt at making &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;in the near-death throes of their existence. Perhaps the fact that it was a failure is even more prescient, given how few bands have been able to take the concept to its logical end in a world full of endless overdubbing and editing temptations.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;After &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;had been rejected at least &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;three times&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;, and the Beatles had all started making their own records, their manager sought to make a little money. Hiring Phil Spector to create an album out of those sessions  especially the rooftop performance  Allan Klein - sometimes credited as acting on behalf of Harrison and Lennon and sometimes not - essentially put together the antithesis of the original album. Now there's all sorts of disagreement about who was in favour of it and who wasn't but the end result was that &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Let It Be &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;definitely wasn't &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;. Yes, a couple of the songs still retain their origins as live-in-the-studio songs sans overdubs but even the ones not obviously effected had studio chatter mixed in from other sessions - as with this version of Get Back, for example. It's hard to know whose album this was but the end result was that McCartney quit the band a week after finding out about it and thereby gained, at least temporarily, the ire of a lot of fans - who were unaware of the number of times the others had quit, or hadn't quite put together that Lennon's burgeoning solo career was leading to this eventually. The only Beatle to actually play on the Spector sessions for the album - in addition to a complete remix of the material, Spector recorded an orchestra and choir - was Starr, who re-recorded his drums for a couple songs. So we know McCartney didnt like it and he was by this point the dominant voice. We know some people claim Lennon liked it  and Lennon did admit in interviews to liking some of what Spector did  but it doesnt fit with anything he was doing musically at the time: his albums with Ono, his first solo album, his singles. Harrison likely approved  whether or not he personally hired Spector for this Beatles album  because he immediately hired Spector to produce his next solo album, the first one to focus on songs. And Starr explicitly approved by being the only Beatle to actually participate in the new recording sessions. It says its a new phase Beatles album on the cover, whatever that means.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;So what do we do with it? Its a Beatles album, as it was released before the official breakup to the group, credited to the group and performed by the group. But it doesnt represent the intentions of the dominant songwriter of the groups late period, and may or may not have represented the intentions of the bands other main songwriter - since he wasn't participating at all at this point - and it doesnt represent the intentions of the groups normal producer either. It is a Beatles album curated by the two least significant Beatles, in one sense. It was only named after the last Beatles single likely because that was the easiest way to move records. Dear Beatles fans. Here is the album to follow up that single you are listening to all the time. It is a back to basics album that is full of overdubs and editing; an utter contradiction.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;But I dont think that invalidates it. Until the release of &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Anthology 3 &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;in 1996 - and really until the release of &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Let It Be Naked &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;in 2003 - this was the only official version of &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;that we ever received. (There have been bootlegs.) And while it is as far from the Beatles intentions with that project as nearly anything could be, it still lets us hear at least some of what would have been absolutely innovative for spring 1969: the first ever back to basics rock album, which has inspired numerous attempts at shedding the studio-as-instrument approach since. And, despite the plethora of covers and jams recorded for the album, and despite the infighting, its not like the band or their lead songwriter took the time off.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;Two of Us is a folk-rock song by McCartney that really does fit the original concept of the abandoned album, only having chatter overdubbed at the beginning to differentiate it from its initial finished version. This final version is slower and softer than earlier run-throughs, but still contains enough charm  I think  and enough of the now typical Beatles unconventionality to appeal: changes in metre, uneven phrasing, and the like; by now standard Beatles tropes. It sounds like little else they had recorded to date. (It actually sounds, to me, like it could have fit in on &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Help! &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;but its more mature stylistically.)&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;Dig a Pony is an impassioned love song by Lennon with mostly nonsense words; he repeatedly dismissed it over the years. Again, it manages to preserve the original feel of the project, despite the remix - which omits two parts of the original version. It is the first of &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Let It Be &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;tracks to be taken from the rooftop concert. Pollack notes:&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;blockquote&gt;The tune makes broad and spicy gestures of contour. The verse starts off with a balanced arch that covers a full octave but ends up with a second upward sweep of that arch left hanging in air, just begging for some release or relief from the refrain. The refrain obligingly picks up where the verse had left things and proceeds to blow the roof off in terms of range; the downbeat of the refrain momentarily establishes a new melodic highpoint just above where the verse tops out, but then, in the second phrase, the tune jumps up practically a full octave to top out in falsetto on the C# eight and a half steps above middle C.&lt;/blockquote&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;Its this kind of rawness that I think discouraged the Beatles from following through with their intentions. They meant to do it initially - or at least McCartney did - but when they sat back and listened, with years of studio perfectionism hanging over their shoulders, they heard mistakes by the ton. Now we may like those mistakes  I do  but we are are also listening in a world where &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Let it Be &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;is a famous record, which was not the world of the decision not to release &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;was made in. &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;This version of Across the Universe was slowed down for an orchestra and choir. The overproduction also includes more effects - in addition to the effects the Beatles added in 1968 - so you cant hear most of the original band, or the fan backing vocals. This version has become a radio staple; it is the version everyone knows and presumably loves. Lennon apparently preferred this one. My attitude is that this song had no place on this record but on the other hand Spector didnt exactly destroy it, like he destroyed some others (see below).&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;The shortest Harrison song that the Beatles ever used  I Me Mine was virtually doubled in length by Spector and also given the full orchestra treatment. It is about ego, apparently. It features some fairly good playing by the composer but it was created completely independently of the &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;project  recorded a full year later  and with full intent to overdub. (There are many even prior to Spectors, as both Harrison and McCartney added additional parts.) Though I am a fan of the music - not of the lyrics - I cant help but think it doesnt belong, despite being the last official Beatles song to be finished.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;Dig It is one of the innumerable jams and song fragments recorded during the &lt;span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;sessions. Why this song was picked over others  and why this version was picked over other, longer versions of Dig It itself  I don't know. Some versions ran to seven minutes but usually broke down. The lyrics changed from version to version - with Lennon spouting off the bands catalogue during one take. It was edited together from two distinct performances by Spector and nobody really knows why. Its one of the reasons this album is looked at as a bit of a mess.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;The album version of Let It Be features the &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;other &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;guitar solo more prominently  Harrison recorded two, an original one and an overdub when they were thinking of releasing it as a single  fewer backing vocals and new and louder orchestration to replace Martin's original, subtle scoring. Most of us Beatles snobs prefer the single version just because. (Really, because it was mostly a band product and it has taste.) I think even with the hatchet job the song still stands up. Its one of the highlights of the album.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;Maggie Mae was the first cover to appear on a Beatles album in years. It was a joke they recorded as one of innumerable covers to warm up. Earlier versions of &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;contained other covers that they attempted more seriously. It is strange that this one was included because it was pretty incomplete compared to many of the other covers they performed back in January of 1969. However, a version of Maggie Mae was included by the Beatles themselves in the planned releases of &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;- though in a different part of the album sequence - so there must be some reason why it is here; some attempt to make a kind of statement about where the band was at. Or just for fun. Or for sequencing purposes. I cant figure it out.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;For me, I've Got a Feeling is one of the highlights of this album. It combines parts of two separate songs - one by McCartney, one by Lennon as was usual by this time - but it is the better for it. Its actually the first time the two had combined song-fragments to create a new song like this since Baby Youre a Rich Man in the summer of 1967  rather than combining whole songs with fragments and the like, as they had on &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;The Beatles &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;and &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Abbey Road&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;. The recording is another rooftop performance and as such, holds at least somewhat with the original concept. Its probably my favourite song on the album and proof, for me, that they were capable of still being a pretty decent live band after years in the studio.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;One After 909 was actually a song from their earliest days. They thought about including it on &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;With the Beatles &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;even though it had been written something like six years prior to those sessions. At this point they were doing their own oldies as well as those of others. It was their attempt to write a train song back in the '50s when such things were popular. The recording is another one from the rooftop concert. Its certainly way louder and rawer than what they attempted in 1963, or 1960 for that matter. But otherwise it does give you a good sense of the kind of problems occurring in the band that January. McCartney contributed the concept and most of the new material. Lennon barely contributed anything. Of the stuff he and Harrison  and even McCartney  did contribute, most of the band couldnt agree on. So they played covers and even resorted to playing songs of their own that they didnt even like. Given that, I am at least impressed by the performance, which is far rawer than the original version - which can be heard on &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Anthology &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;along with many of the outtakes I am referring to.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;The Long and Winding Road is the most controversial track on the album as it features the most glaring intervention from Spector along with that on I Me Mine: a huge orchestra and choir and effects. It was one of the main reasons why McCartney publicly quit when he found out about the release of this album. If you think it's sappy you should listen to the &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Anthology &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;version which is considerably less so. The version we all know is not the right version but theres no way around that now. Its even harder for us to go back and erase, as it were, our memories of this version to hear the original, to decide whether it is actually not a sappy ballad, but rather a good ballad. I dont know that Ive been able to do that. It was supposedly an attempt to write in the style of Ray Charles poppier ballads.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;For You Blue is Harrison trying to write a traditional blues song - with not so bluesy lyrics - and it features Lennon's debut on the lap-steel - a steel guitar played on the lap, funnily enough. Its as straightforward for the Beatles as it gets. But its still relatively charming. Harrison re-recorded his vocal but the chatter was put in by Spector. At least it doesnt feature an orchestra for no apparent reason like the other Harrison contribution. I think Lennon acquits himself fairly well on the lap steel, which is somewhat of a surprise.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;The album version of Get Back features all sorts of dialogue not recorded at the time. Otherwise it is actually the same as the single released a year previously - just a remix. So theres not much to choose from between the two versions, which begs the question, why did Spector include the single version of this song when he created a new version of Let It Be? I dont know. I dont think anyone does. &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: normal; margin-bottom: .0001pt; margin-bottom: 0cm; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Let It Be &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;was the first thing the Beatles released in ages  except &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Yellow Submarine &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;of course  that can really be criticized by the standards of the time, and can be considered inessential in terms of rock music history. &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;would have stood up amongst their best work  I am convinced  as yet another path-breaking record featuring (mostly) strong songs. But &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Let It Be &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;is not &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Get Back&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;. And as such, it has to be viewed somewhat as the group's nadir - though, I think, &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Magical Mystery Tour &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: #000081; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;could also be viewed that way&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;. But as a nadir, it still features a lot of great music and four songs that have become part of the canon of radio staples, if not standards out right. Pick your favourite band from the '60s and compare &lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPS-ItalicMT;"&gt;Let It Be &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;to the worst album of their career. Who wins? I think you're lying &lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: #000081; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;if you say it isn't the Beatles&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT; font-size: 12.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: TimesNewRomanPSMT;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://www.amazon.ca/dp/B00KUPGLJ4" target="_blank"&gt;Read my book about the Beatles&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</content><link href="http://superchicken46.blogspot.com/feeds/4309108753175363519/comments/default" rel="replies" title="Post Comments" scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="2004"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="2010"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="30 term="Yankees"></category><title type="text">30 for 30: Four Days in October (2010, Gary Waksman)</title><content type="html">I hate the Red Sox. I mean, I fucking hate them. Though not as much as I hate the Yankees. But I feel like I remember this series like it was yesterday. (But I more remember where I was than the actual moments of the game.) Among the best baseball playoffs I've seen in my adult life. (There have been a few that were better in my mind, such as Diamondbacks / Yankees, but not many.) This was a great series and the film does a good job of getting us to  understand how incredible it was, in part by focusing on the players,  for the most part. Millar in particular seems to have had boundless  confidence and it helps that the players documented it themselves.&lt;br /&gt;One huge problem with the film is its self-importance. The opening credits are ridiculous (and rival pretty much only &lt;i&gt;June 17, 1994&lt;/i&gt; in the series for ridiculousness) and the music is often utterly over the top (not to mention cliche) - it actually descends into parody.&lt;br /&gt;Another weird thing is the teeny tiny bits of Bill Simmons "interviewing" comedian Lenny Clarke, I guess trying to provide context for those who didn't watch it, but they're so brief and so intermittent that's hard to know why they included them instead of, say, narration. (Well, we know why, Simmons is the exec producer. But still...)&lt;br /&gt;But this is another one of those films where the subject matter overcomes clunky direction. What a great series.&lt;br /&gt;7/10</content><link href="http://superchicken46.blogspot.com/feeds/6665753738966488688/comments/default" rel="replies" title="Post Comments" value="FILTERED_POSTMOD"></gd:extendedproperty></entry><entry><id>tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-8545641.post-1957182493579968941</id><published>2015-05-10T12:40:00.003-04:00</published><updated>2015-05-10T12:40:47.145-04:00</updated><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="2010"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="30 for 30"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Canada"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Cancer"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Documentary"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Running"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Terry Fox"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="TV"></category><title type="text">30 for 30: Into the Wind (2010, Ezra Holland, Steve Nash)</title><content type="html">If you grew up in Canada in the 1980s, you know Terry Fox and his story. He ran the year before I was born, but despite that, I know the legend/myth just like most Canadians my age. Here's the real connection I have to the story:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-dIxGHX37GFc/VU-HI-hrGvI/AAAAAAAAAT4/YgMlawyioms/s1600/F1000022.JPG" imageanchor="1" style="margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="214" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-dIxGHX37GFc/VU-HI-hrGvI/AAAAAAAAAT4/YgMlawyioms/s320/F1000022.JPG" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;This is the view from the memorial outside of Thunder Bay. I apologize for the shitty picture. I didn't take a picture of the memorial...because? I guess there wasn't a view...&lt;br /&gt;But I didn't know the story this well, so this was still a new experience for me. It's interesting to see how different something like this was before the internet. It's incredible to me that people didn't know about this when he started, and it's also worth thinking about how nothing has really changed - people always want to use things for their own purposes, be it political gain, money, or other things.&lt;br /&gt;I guess because this is such a fundamental Canadian myth for those of us my age, that I was barely able to keep myself together while watching it. (A hangover might have helped with that.) I have no idea if it's this moving for non-Canadians, but seeing the full story is kind of overwhelming: I'm filled with pride, sadness, and a whole host of other emotions.&lt;br /&gt;The film itself is far from perfect. And that makes sense given Nash and his co-director's lack of filmmaking experience. But despite some clunky moments - particularly in the early goings, when some of the montages and shots are really, really cliche and/or weak - the story is so powerful (at least to a Canadian) that you really don't care.&lt;br /&gt;8/10&lt;br /&gt;PS Good job Quebec. Way to not fight cancer.</content><link href="http://superchicken46.blogspot.com/feeds/1957182493579968941/comments/default" rel="replies" title="Post Comments" type="application/atom+xml"/><link href="http://www.blogger.com/comment.g?blogID=8545641&amp;postID=1957182493579968941&amp;isPopup=true" rel="replies" title="0 Comments" type="text/html"/><link name="commentModerationMode" value="FILTERED_POSTMOD"></gd:extendedproperty></entry><entry><id>tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-8545641.post-4949360158963721832</id><published>2015-05-10T11:22:00.002-04:00</published><updated>2015-05-10T12:24:09.712-04:00</updated><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="2010"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="30 for 30"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Baseball"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Documentary"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="New York"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Sports"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="TV"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Yankees"></category><title type="text">30 for 30: The House of Steinbrenner (2010, Barbara Kopple)</title><content type="html">This is a pretty bloodless and blah attempt to document the mixed (mostly) sad emotions of moving from the old Yankee Stadium to the new one. At times it also feels like it's about the Steinbrenners. There are plenty of interviews with people affected by this change, there are the famous clips, there's talk about how they spend money, but it doesn't really add up. But they don't dwell very long on Steinbrenner's terrible record as an owner before he was suspended (that gets maybe 5 minutes) and the filmmakers seem unsure whether this is about the move, about the Steinbrenners or about the Yankees. It tries to be all of these things and unfortunately that gives us a really unbalanced film that fails to make me care about this move, &lt;i&gt;at all&lt;/i&gt;. (That should be the challenge, after all. Making this interesting for non-Yankee fans.) Very, very blah.&lt;br /&gt;5/10</content><link href="http://superchicken46.blogspot.com/feeds/4949360158963721832/comments/default" rel="replies" title="Post Comments" scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="1770s"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="2011"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Classical"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Music"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="String Quartet"></category><title type="text">Joseph Haydn: String Quartets Op. 20 'Sun' (2011) by the London Haydn Quartet</title><content type="html">As with his symphonies and some of his other works, Haydn wrote a ton of String Quartets. Just an absolute ton. This set collects the 23rd through 28th, all of which were written at the same time, as one cycle or collection. They are considered by most people to be the birth of the modern String Quartet and wikipedia tells me that the form established here didn't really change for 200 years, which is insane.&lt;br /&gt;I am a sucker for String Quartets and I can't say that I've heard any earlier than these in my life. So earlier Quartets would probably strike my ears as exceptionally primitive.&lt;br /&gt;I am tempted to write down my thoughts about all of them but other people have said it much better than me:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;blockquote&gt;This cannot be overstated. The six string quartets of Opus 20 are as important in the history of music, and had as radically a transforming effect on the very field of musical possibility itself, as Beethoven's Third Symphony would 33 years later.&lt;br /&gt;- Ron Drummond&lt;/blockquote&gt;&lt;blockquote&gt;Every page of the six quartets of op. 20 is of historic and aesthetic importance... there is perhaps no single or sextuple opus in the history of instrumental music which has achieved so much.&lt;br /&gt;- Donald Tovey&lt;/blockquote&gt;I cannot add to that.&lt;br /&gt;10/10&lt;br /&gt;PS The beginning of one of the repeating phrases in the second of these quartets sounds exactly like one of the themes from the &lt;i&gt;Star Wars&lt;/i&gt; score.</content><link scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="1780s"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="2014"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Classical"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Music"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Oratorio"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="String Quartet"></category><title type="text">Joseph Haydn: Die Sieben letzten Worte (2014) by Cuarteto Casals</title><content type="html">This is supposedly an "instrumental" oratorio. Haydn first wrote it for orchestra (with no vocals!). Then he adapted it for String Quartet. Then he adapted it for Choir (as if it was an actual oratorio). Then he "approved" an adaptation for solo piano, but apparently didn't write that one himself. This is the String Quartet version, obviously. It is considered the most popular version of the piece, which I guess makes me okay with listening to it over the orchestral original.&lt;br /&gt;I am a sucker for String Quartets and Haydn is the Father of them. (He didn't invent them, but he was the first composer to write a lot of them, and to establish the conventions of the genre.) Given the piece's origins, it is not a conventional Quartet: it's nine movements long, for example, instead of the usual three. The music is played every Holy Week in Cadiz, and has been for centuries apparently, so it makes sense to hear a Spanish ensemble's version.&lt;br /&gt;I have no idea what this is &lt;i&gt;supposed&lt;/i&gt; to sound like but I absolutely love this recording. It strikes me as rather sophisticated for its era - it sort of sounds like a collection of separate pieces, like a cycle - and my ears would have a hard time dating it, if I didn't know when it was written. The music itself is gorgeous and I can understand why it's so popular.&lt;br /&gt;It's an absolute masterpiece, and I have trouble putting into words how much I like it.&lt;br /&gt;10/10</content><link href="http://superchicken46.blogspot.com/feeds/4815954421866575603/comments/default" rel="replies" title="Post Comments" type="application/atom+xml"/><link scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Sports"></category><title type="text">30 for 30: June 17th, 1994 (2010, Brett Morgen)</title><content type="html">This is a fascinating, ambitious but flawed attempt at putting the OJ Simpson chase into some kind of greater sports context, and perhaps to serve as a document for those people who were not alive to witness this.&lt;br /&gt;Like most people my age and older, I remember this event rather well. What I didn't remember was how it corresponded with the Rangers' Cup parade (I had stopped watching hockey the previous year), Game 5 of the NBA Finals (I didn't watch basketball at the time), Arnold Palmer's last time at the US Open (golf is terrible), the opening of the World Cup of Soccer (soccer is terrible, but not as terrible as golf) and some baseball games (finally something I cared about!). The film does a good job of showing how important this seemed - I can't tell whether it's mocking that sense of import or really taking it so seriously. In retrospect to me it is absolutely shocking that they interrupted a game of the NBA Finals to show a &lt;i&gt;car chase&lt;/i&gt;, as if the car chase was September 11th or something. (Obviously they couldn't know that, but it's incredible the lack of proportion the TV media showed that day.)&lt;br /&gt;And it's interesting to think of how this changed how we watched TV. Other people have suggested how the OJ chase and trial started sensationalist television in the United States. I don't know if that's true, but it definitely feels like it was a major tipping point along the path to where we are now, where non news dominates news all the time.&lt;br /&gt;The film captures a lot of this and would be a masterpiece only it's a little too self-important (the opening feels like I'm about to watch the most important movie ever) and it's attempt at artfulness are often slightly more awkward than they should be. scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Human Rights"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Movies"></category><title type="text">Ai Weiwei: Never Sorry (2012, Alison Klayman)</title><content type="html">I had no idea who Ai Weiwei was until I saw his show at the AGO maybe two years ago. I can't say I'm up on contemporary art but I was really impressed at both the ballsiness and the variety of his work. This film is more about his role as a political dissident in China, though it is also about his career as a celebrity artist. The documentary is pretty biased, but that's not a big deal given what he is up to. Basically, even if you don't like his art, if you think he is some kind of charlatan or artistic fraud, you can't ignore what he's doing to bring attention to what is happening in China or how he has turned twitter into a tool for social protest and awareness to a greater extent than most people.&lt;br /&gt;This documentary is flawed - it uses the stupid "camera shutter click" noise &lt;i&gt;way&lt;/i&gt; too much for example - but the subject is compelling enough, and the documentary is chronological enough that it doesn't feel as directionless as its style and form at first suggest.&lt;br /&gt;Certainly of interest if you are interested in Ai, if you are interested in China, or if you are interested in the intersection of art and politics.&lt;br /&gt;7/10</content><link href="http://superchicken46.blogspot.com/feeds/7607473882027881829/comments/default" rel="replies" title="Post Comments" type="application/atom+xml"/><link href="http://www.blogger.com/comment.g?blogID=8545641&amp;postID=7607473882027881829&amp;isPopup=true" rel="replies" title="0 Comments" type="text/html"/><link scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Basketball"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Draft"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Greatest of All Time"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Hall of Fame"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="NBA"></category><category scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="Raptors"></category><title type="text">The Top 5 Raptors Draft Picks of All Time</title><content type="html">The other day, I covered 10 of the "best" 15 draft picks the Raptors have made. Today we'll be talking about the Top 5, the 5 players the Raptors have drafted, a couple of whom might actually make it into the Hall of Fame some day. I will be using a different format for covering these players, as they all have (sometimes quite weak) Hall of Fame cases, which can't be said of any of the players covered in the earlier post.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;a href="http://superchicken46.blogspot.ca/2015/04/raptors-best-draft-picks.html" target="_blank"&gt;See picks 6-16&lt;/a&gt; &lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;a name='more'&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;h4&gt;5. Damon Stoudamire, PG, drafted 7th overall, 1995&lt;/h4&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Career:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;13 seasons, 10 quality&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;33.2 MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;11,763P, 5,371A, 953S, 3,039R, 81B in 29,106 minutes over 878 games &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 14.5P, 6.6A, 1.2S, 3.8R, 1.B&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.406 FG%, .357 3P%, .833 FT%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;15.4 PER, .505 TS%, 55.4 Win Shares, .091 WS per 48, 15.4 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Playoffs:&lt;/li&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;28.2 MPG &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;533P, 233A, 32S, 159R, 9B in 1,496 minutes over 53 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 12.8P, 5.6A, 1S, 3.8R, .2B&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.399 FG%, .389 3P%, .847 FT%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;13.3 PER, .496 TS%, 1.6 WS, .052 WS per 48, 0.6 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;Traded once in his prime&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Accomplishments&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in minutes once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in MPG twice, Top 10 thrice &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in 3 Pointers thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in 3 Point Attempts once, Top 10 thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Assists twice, Top 10 four times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in FT% twice, Top 10 thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in APG once, Top 10 thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Assist %age once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;All Rookie&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Great Teams:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&amp;nbsp;Starting point guard on two Final Fours ('99, '00 Blazers)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;Drafted immediately after Bryant Reeves (phew!) and a few spots ahead of Kurt Thomas; other NBA players drafted below Stoudamire include Brent Barry (15th), Theo Ratliff (18th), Michael Finley (21st) and others. In his draft year, Stoudamire is:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;9th in games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;5th&lt;/b&gt; in minutes&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;6th in points&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;12th in boards&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;2nd&lt;/b&gt; in assists&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;31st in FG% (min 100 games)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in 3P% (ditto)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in FT% (ditto)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;5th&lt;/b&gt; in PPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;16th in RPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;1st&lt;/b&gt; in APG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;8th in WS&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;16th in WS per 48 (min 100 games)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;Mighty Mouse definitely turned out to be a great pick in his draft class, turning into one of the better players to enter the NBA that year. But, though he is fondly remembered by fans, Stoudamire was far from an All-Time Great and could see his spot on this list usurped by DeRozan or Hibbert pretty soon.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;b&gt;How did he get away?&lt;/b&gt; Traded with Carlos Rogers and Walt Williams to the Trail Blazers for Kenny Anderson, Gary Trent, Alvin Williams, two 1st Rounders in 1998 (Bryce Drew, Mirsad Turkcan), and a 2nd Rounder in 1998 (Tyson Wheeler). So, uh, that was a massive deal. Kind of eel like maybe we won it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h4&gt;4. Antawn Jamison, SF/PF, 4th overall, 1998&lt;/h4&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Career:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;16 seasons, 15 quality&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;34.8 MPG &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;20,042P, 8,157R, 1,761A, 1,048S, 421B in 37,638 minutes over 1,083 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 19.2P, 7.8R, 1.7A, 1S, .4B&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; All Time in Turnover %age&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.451 FG%, .346 3P%, .724 FT% &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;18.1 PER, .523 TS%, 87.8 Win Shares, .112 WS per 48, 17.4 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Playoffs:&lt;/li&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;34.9 MPG &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;791P, 330, 58A, 36S, 33B in 1606 minutes over 46 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 17.7P, 7.4R, 1.3A, .8S, .7B&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.448 FG%, .341 3P%, .706 FT%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;16.5 PER, .524 TS%, 3.1 WS, .093 WS per 48, 0.3 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;Traded the day he was drated, traded twice in his prime, twice after, and waived at the end of his career&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Accomplishments:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;6th Man of the Year ('04)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 Offensive Player by Offensive Rating ('04) &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in minutes played thrice, Top 10 four times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in MPG once, Top 10 thrice &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Points thrice&amp;nbsp;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in PPG once &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Field Goals twice, Top 10 thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in FGA twice, Top 10 five times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in FTA once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Offensive Rebounds once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Defensive Rebounds thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 Rebounds thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in RPG once &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in FG% once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in TS% once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Turnover % six times, Top 10 eleven times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Offensive Win Shares twice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;2 All Star Games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;2nd All Rookie &lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Great Teams:&lt;/h3&gt;None&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Drafted immediately behind Raef LaFrentz and immediately ahead of Vince Carter; other NBA players he was drafted ahead of include Dirk (9th), Pierce (10th), Al Harrington (25th), Rashard Lewis (32nd) and lots of others (it was a deep draft). In his draft year, Jamison is:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in minutes&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in points&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;2nd&lt;/b&gt; in boards&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;11th in assists&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;17th in FG% (min 100 games)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;17th in 3P% (ditto)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;23rd in FT% (ditto)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;5th&lt;/b&gt; in MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in PPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;2nd&lt;/b&gt; in RPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;19th in APG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;5th&lt;/b&gt; in Win Shares&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;8th in WS per 48&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;Jamison is pretty close to the 4th best player in his draft and he was drafted 4th overall. Unfortunately for him, the 5th overall player had the better career. Fortunately for the Raptors, they immediately traded Jamison for that player, Vince.&lt;br /&gt;Jamison has had an odd career: he's got enough longevity that some people will likely argue that he belongs in the Hall of Fame (despite his poor luck at almost always playing on bad teams), on the other hand by some metrics (VORP), Camby was more important to his teams than Jamison, that's just one example of likely a large number of players.&lt;br /&gt;Jamison was unlucky in who he played for, and that will likely affect the perception of his legacy of a good but not quite ever great player.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;b&gt;How did he get away?&lt;/b&gt; Traded to the Warriors for Vince Carter plus cash immediately after getting drafted. Oops. (That's an oops for Golden State.)&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;h4&gt;3. Marcus Camby, C, drafted 2nd overall, 1996&lt;/h4&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Career:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;17 seasons, 14 quality&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;29.5 MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;9,262P, 9,513R, 2,331B (13th all time), 1,837A, 967S in 28,684 minutes over 973 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 11.6P, 11.9R, 2.9B, 2.3A, 1.2S&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;14th All Time in BPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;8th All Time in Block Percentage&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;16th All Time in Defensive Rating&amp;nbsp;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; All Time in Defensive Box Plus Minus&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.466 FG%, .205 3P%, .670 FT%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;17.8 PER, .503 TS%, 81.6 Win Shares, .137 WS per 48, 35.6 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Playoffs:&lt;/li&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;28.4 MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;589P, 712R, 151B, 95A, 61S in 2,245 minutes over 79 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 9.4P, 11.4R, 2.4B, 1.5A, 1S&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.443 FG%, .429 3P% (on 7 shots), .578 FT%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;15.6 PER, .473 TS%, 5.4 WS, .116 WS per 48, 1.7 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;Traded 7 times but only once in his prime&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Accomplishments:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;Best Defensive Player by Defensive Rating ('01), Top 5 five times ('00, '05, '06, '07), Top 10 ('99, '08, '10, '11, '12)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Best Defensive Player by Defensive Box Plus Minus five times ('05,  '08, '10, '11, '12), Top 5 ten times ('01, '04, '06, '07, '09), Top 10  eleven times ('00)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Offensive Rebounds once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Defensive Rebounds thrice, Top 10 five times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Rebounds twice, Top 10 thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in RPG thrice, Top 10 four times &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Offensive Rebound %age thrice, Top 10 four times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Led the league in Defensive Rebound %age thrice, Top 5 eleven times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Led the league in Rebound %age four times, Top 5 eight times, Top 10 ten times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Led the league in blocks thrice, Top 5 five times, Top 10 seven times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Led the league in BPG four times, Top 5 eight times, Top 10 ten times &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Led the league in Block %age thrice, Top 5 seven times, Top 10 fourteen times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in FG% and 2P FG% once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Turnover %age once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Offensive Rating once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Defensive Win Shares once, Top 10 thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Win Shares per 48 Minutes once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in VORP once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;2 1st All Defense, 2 2nd Team, All Rookie &lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Great Teams:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;"Backup" centre (but he played more minutes than the starter) on one  Runner Up ('99 Knicks) and (real) backup centre on one Final Four ('00  Knicks)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;Camby was drafted immediately behind Iversion and immediately  ahead of Shareef Abdul-Rahim, Starbury, Ray Allen and Atoine Walker;  other players drafted below Camby include Kobe (13th), Peja Stojakovic  (14th), Nash (15th), Jermaine O'Neal (17th), Zydrunas Ilgauskus (20th).  In his draft year, Camby is:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;7th in games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;9th in minutes&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;12th in points&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;1st&lt;/b&gt; in boards&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;10th in assists&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;10th in FG% (min 100 games)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;24th in 3P% (ditto)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;25th in FT% (ditto)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;10th in MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;12th in PPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;1st&lt;/b&gt; in RPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;13th in APG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;6th in Win Shares&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;7th in WS per 48&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;By how he compares to his draft class in he above categories,  Camby looks like a bit of a semi-bust, not a full on bust, but a least a  semi-bust. And that's probably why he wasn't included on that list on  TSN.ca. But these are almost entirely offensive categories. Defense  isn't mentioned (BPG for example) except for rebounds, which are about  2/3 defensive and 1/3 offensive usually. If we just focus on the above  numbers, and not on some other notable things, Camby looks pedestrian  and a bad pick at 2. (Obviously if the draft could be done in the full  possession of knowledge of the future, the 13th overall player would  have gone 1st.)&lt;br /&gt;But, though he never won the DPOY, Camby was, by  two separate metrics, among the best defenders in the league, perhaps  even the best, for a number of years. Unfortunately for Camby, the way  we think about defense has evolved only after his prime, so he will  likely not get a lot of respect from HOF voters. He should get a least  some respect from us, and until Hibbert gets a few more (consistent)  years under his belt, Camby stands as the best defender the Raps ever  drafted.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;b&gt;How did he get away?&lt;/b&gt; Traded to the Knicks for Sean Marks, Charles Oakley and cashmoney. Ooof.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;h4&gt;2. Chris Bosh, PF/C, drafted 4th overall, 2003&lt;/h4&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Career:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;12 seasons, all quality&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;35.9 MPG &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;16,179P, 7,202R, 898B, 1,667A, 694S in 30,158 minutes over 840 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 19.3P, 8.6R, 1.1B, 2A, .8S&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.495 FG%, .326 3P%, .799 FT%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;20.6 PER, .571 TS%, 99.6 WS, .159 WS per 48 minutes, 25.6 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Playoffs:&lt;/li&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;35.2 MPG &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;1,388P, 665R, 102B, 119A, 73S in 3,131 minutes over 89 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 16P, 7.6R, 1.2B, 1.4A, .8S&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.473 FG%, .386 3P%, .8 FT%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;18.4 PER, .553 TS%, 9.4 Win Shares, .144 WS per 48, 2.4 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;Rights traded once in his prime&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Accomplishments:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 player by PER ('10), Top 10 twice ('08)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 Offensive Player by Offensive Rating once ('06)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in MPG once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Usage %age twice &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Points twice&amp;nbsp;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in PPG thrice &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Free Throws once, Top 10 four times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in FTA once, Top 10 four times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Defensive Rebounds thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Rebounds once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in RPG twice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Offensive Win Shares once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;10 All Star Games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;1 2nd Team All Star, All Rookie &lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;&amp;nbsp;Great Teams:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;3rd best player on two Champions ('12, '13 Heat) and two Runners Up ('11, '14 Heat)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Olympic Champion ('08)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;World Champion Bronze Medallist ('06)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Drafted immediately behind Melo and immediately ahead of Wade and Chris Kaman; other NBA players drafted below Bosh include David West (18th), Josh Howard (29th), Mo Williams (47th), Kyle Korver (51st) and lots of others. In his draft year, Bosh is:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in minutes&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in points&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;1st&lt;/b&gt; in boards&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;11th in assists&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in FG% (min 100 games)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;24th in 3P%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;11th in FT% (min 100 games)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in PPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;1st&lt;/b&gt; in RPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;14th in APG (min 100 games)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in Win Shares&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in WS per 48 minutes&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;Just like Jamison, Bosh has been outshone by the player drafted immediately below him. However, unlike Jamison, Bosh's fate is now tied to Wade's, so it's harder to compare and contrast the two. This draft is widely held to be one of the greatest ever, so it's a testament to his career that Bosh comes out as the 3rd or 4th best player regardless of where he was drafted. (Who'd rather have had Melo at this point?) Also, Bosh is the first person on this list we can say with pretty good certainty is going to the Hall of Fame.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;b&gt;How did he get away?&lt;/b&gt; Rights traded to the Heat for two 2011 1st Rounders (Norris Cole, Valanciunas).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;h4&gt;1. Tracy McGrady, SG/SF, drafted 9th overall, 1997&lt;/h4&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Career: &lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;15 seasons, 13 quality&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;32.7 MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;18,381P, 4,161A, 1,136S, 5,276R, 807B in 30,658 minutes over 938 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 19.6P, 4.4A, 1.2S, 5.6R, .9B&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;13th All Time in Usage %age&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;21st All Time in Box Plus Minus&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;21st All time in Offensive Box Plus Minus&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.435 FG%, .338 3P%, .746 FT%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;22.1 PER, .519 TS%, 97.3 WS, .152 WS per 48, 50.6 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Playoffs:&lt;/li&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;34.5 MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;1,109P, 249A, 53S, 286R, 47B in 1,724 minutes over 50 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Per 36: 23.2P, 5.2A, 1.1S, 6R, 1B&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.426 FG%, .29 3P%, .757 FT%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;23.4 PER, .512 TS%, 4.5 WS, .125 WS per 48, 1.8 VORP&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;Traded twice in his prime and once after&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;&amp;nbsp;Accomplishments:&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;Most Improved Player ('01)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Best Player by PER ('03), Top 5 ('01, '02, '04)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Best Player by Win Shares Per 48 Minutes ('03)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Best Player by Box Plus Minus ('03)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Best Offensive Player by Offensive Box Plus Minus ('03, '04)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 Player by VORP ('02, '03, '04, '05), Top 10 ('01, '07) &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Minutes once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in MPG once, Top 10 twice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Led the league in Usage %age twice, Top 5 six times, Top 10 seven times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Points thrice, Top 10 six times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Led league in PPG twice, Top 5 thrice, Top 10 eight times &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Field Goals five times, Top 10 six times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in FGA six times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in 3 Pointers twice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in 3PA twice, Top 10 thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Free Throws once Top 10 four times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5in FTA once, Top 10 four times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Assist %age once &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Steals once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in SPG once &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Blocks once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 10 in Turnover %age once &lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Led the league in Offensive Win Shares once, Top 10 four times&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Defensive Win Shares once&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;Top 5 in Win Shares once, Top 10 thrice&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;7 All Star Game appearances&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;2 1st Team All Stars, 3 2nd Team, 2 3rd Team&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;h3&gt;Great Teams:&lt;/h3&gt;None&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Drafted immediately behind Adonal Foyle and immediately ahead of Danny Fortson. (Phew.) Notable NBA player drafted below McGrady begin and end with Stephen Jackson (42nd). In his draft year, McGrady is:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in minutes&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;2nd&lt;/b&gt; in points&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;2nd&lt;/b&gt; in boards&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in assists&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;17th in FG% (min 100 games)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;14th in 3P% (ditto)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;21st in FT% (ditto)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;2nd&lt;/b&gt; in MPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;1st&lt;/b&gt; in PPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in RPG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in APG&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;3rd&lt;/b&gt; in Win Shares&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;4th&lt;/b&gt; in WS per 48 (min 100 games)&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;So, um, that was the right choice.&amp;nbsp; The only reason T-Mac wasn't the best player in his draft is because Tim Duncan went first overall that year. The only reason he looks like the 3rd best player is because Chauncey Billups had fewer injury problems and was lucky enough to get traded to the Pistons.&lt;br /&gt;The idea that the Raps have drafted a better player than T-Mac is patently absurd. Bosh is great, but he was never this dominant. And nobody else is worth mentioning. and in case all you remember is poor old beaten-up-by-injuries, just look at this:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;2,407P, 411A, 124S, 488R, 59B in 2954 minutes over 75 games&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;.457 FG%, .386 3P%, .793 FT%, .564 TS%&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;30.3 PER&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;This is &lt;a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/play-index/psl_finder.cgi?request=1&amp;amp;match=single&amp;amp;type=totals&amp;amp;per_minute_base=36&amp;amp;per_poss_base=100&amp;amp;lg_id=NBA&amp;amp;is_playoffs=N&amp;amp;year_min=&amp;amp;year_max=&amp;amp;franch_id=&amp;amp;season_start=1&amp;amp;season_end=-1&amp;amp;age_min=0&amp;amp;age_max=99&amp;amp;height_min=0&amp;amp;height_max=99&amp;amp;shoot_hand=&amp;amp;birth_country_is=Y&amp;amp;birth_country=&amp;amp;birth_state=&amp;amp;college_id=&amp;amp;is_active=&amp;amp;debut_yr_nba_start=&amp;amp;debut_yr_nba_end=&amp;amp;debut_yr_aba_start=&amp;amp;debut_yr_aba_end=&amp;amp;is_hof=&amp;amp;is_as=&amp;amp;as_comp=gt&amp;amp;as_val=&amp;amp;award=&amp;amp;pos_is_g=Y&amp;amp;pos_is_gf=Y&amp;amp;pos_is_f=Y&amp;amp;pos_is_fg=Y&amp;amp;pos_is_fc=Y&amp;amp;pos_is_c=Y&amp;amp;pos_is_cf=Y&amp;amp;qual=&amp;amp;c1stat=g&amp;amp;c1comp=gt&amp;amp;c1val=41&amp;amp;c2stat=per&amp;amp;c2comp=gt&amp;amp;c2val=30&amp;amp;c3stat=&amp;amp;c3comp=gt&amp;amp;c3val=&amp;amp;c4stat=&amp;amp;c4comp=gt&amp;amp;c4val=&amp;amp;c5stat=&amp;amp;c5comp=gt&amp;amp;c6mult=1.0&amp;amp;c6stat=&amp;amp;order_by=per" target="_blank"&gt;the 17th greatest offensive regular season in NBA history&lt;/a&gt;. It happened in 2002-03. It was T-Mac at his best. People will say T-Mac never won a playoff series and so somehow he wasn't that great. That's stupid, particularly in a 30 team league and particularly given the injury history of both T-Mac and his best teammates. And &lt;a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/teams/ORL/2003.html" target="_blank"&gt;look at his team that year&lt;/a&gt;, what the hell was he supposed to do with that?&lt;br /&gt;There is zero debate about who the best Raptors draft pick of all time is, despite what TSN would tell you.&lt;br /&gt;There shouldn't be much debate about whether or not T-Mac makes the Hall of Fame.&lt;br scheme="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#" term="The Band"></category><title type="text">Ain't in It for My Health (2010, Jacob Hatley)</title><content type="html">This is a kind of directionless but, at times, moving portrait of part of one of the last years of Levon Helm's life. He's struggling to continue to perform music while he deals with what might be a return of his throat cancer. The film isn't really sure what it wants to be: we get a portrait of him at home, a little bit of him on the road, some history about The Band and his feelings toward some of the members, but all of this feels like the filmmaker is just sort of probing here and there. Though seeing into the life of a famous musician this close to death is a rare thing, the film still feels like a missed opportunity. I feel like all I got from it was the reassurance that Helm was a character and that he loved what he did. And I knew both of those things going in.&lt;br /&gt;6/10</content><link href="http://superchicken46.blogspot.com/feeds/7087685332119286562/comments/default" rel="replies" title="Post Comments"
